              <span id="reviewTextContainer415791558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3060083438218244108">Having not fallen fully under the sway of Dickens’s longest, <i>Bleak House</i>, we’re back to the savagely impressive corkers with this satirical and tender effort from the Immortal Blighty Scribe (IBS—unfortunate acronym). On a less grandiose scale than the preceding tome, <i>Little Dorrit</i> is much quieter, funnier, more powerfully affecting novel throughout than <i>BH</i>. In two parts, Poverty & Riches, the novel charts the progress of Amy Dorrit, (the token spirit of purity and goodness), and her family from</span>
  <span id="freeText3060083438218244108" style="display:none">Having not fallen fully under the sway of Dickens’s longest, <i>Bleak House</i>, we’re back to the savagely impressive corkers with this satirical and tender effort from the Immortal Blighty Scribe (IBS—unfortunate acronym). On a less grandiose scale than the preceding tome, <i>Little Dorrit</i> is much quieter, funnier, more powerfully affecting novel throughout than <i>BH</i>. In two parts, Poverty &amp; Riches, the novel charts the progress of Amy Dorrit, (the token spirit of purity and goodness), and her family from Marshelsea debtors’ prison into a shaky life of infinite riches and never-ending Italian holidays. Central to the novel is her father William, who replaces his memories of destitution with violent hauteur, and whose mental collapse is rendered with masterful swings of wrenching drama. Clenham is the more complex, reticent hero, almost frustratingly dim in spots, but no less than impeccable on the moral scruples front. Apart from a sudden gallop into action-packed melodrama in the last 100pp or so, and a byzantine final-reveal sequence to out-<i>Lost Lost</i>, <i>Little Dorrit</i> goes straight atop the essential-Dickens pile, along with all the others. [And a final warning to Oxford World’s Classics: if you make your fonts any smaller, I will send in the midget assassins].<br /><br /><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QIaZkiX7SPY" rel="nofollow noopener">Recent Andrew Davies BBC version on YT</a></span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61989141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7489115338857482679"><i>Little Dorrit</i> by Charles Dickens is arguably one of the very best fiction books I've read in my entire life. I would unhesitatingly recommend this book to anyone. It was captivating, engaging, and at times humorous, and at other times sad; with romance, mystery, and intrigue. Dickens' plotting is amazing, his characters intriguing, and his descriptions solidly place you in the midst of London in the Victorian Age in all social classes. The message and moral tone of this novel is so incredibly ap</span>
  <span id="freeText7489115338857482679" style="display:none"><i>Little Dorrit</i> by Charles Dickens is arguably one of the very best fiction books I've read in my entire life. I would unhesitatingly recommend this book to anyone. It was captivating, engaging, and at times humorous, and at other times sad; with romance, mystery, and intrigue. Dickens' plotting is amazing, his characters intriguing, and his descriptions solidly place you in the midst of London in the Victorian Age in all social classes. The message and moral tone of this novel is so incredibly applicable to today's economic and social conditions. A fabulous book; and made even more fabulous with watching the sumptuous Andrew Davies screenplay brought to life in the multi-episode BBC adaptation that aired originally in March-April 2009 on Masterpiece Classics on PBS TV. I think that <i>Little Dorrit</i> is an important book, not only for our time, but anytime, and a book that I simply love to revisit every few years.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_12619500" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12619500" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12619500?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/40114569-little-dorrit">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12619500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18043848403731105467">Though the title character is static, never wavering, by the end, she has transformed into a symbol. I was reminded of the title character in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17150.My__ntonia__Great_Plains_Trilogy___3_' title='My Ántonia (Great Plains Trilogy, #3) by Willa Cather' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Ántonia</a> in that she too becomes a symbol by her book’s end—a symbol of an ideal American woman. In much the same way, Amy Dorrit is the symbol of ideal English womanhood, at least in the eyes of the time period: taking care of her difficult father, always with patience and love; sticking by her man, doing all for him, even when he’s not aware of it. <br/><br/>Tho</span>
  <span id="freeText18043848403731105467" style="display:none">Though the title character is static, never wavering, by the end, she has transformed into a symbol. I was reminded of the title character in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17150.My__ntonia__Great_Plains_Trilogy___3_" title="My Ántonia (Great Plains Trilogy, #3) by Willa Cather" rel="nofollow noopener">My Ántonia</a> in that she too becomes a symbol by her book’s end—a symbol of an ideal American woman. In much the same way, Amy Dorrit is the symbol of ideal English womanhood, at least in the eyes of the time period: taking care of her difficult father, always with patience and love; sticking by her man, doing all for him, even when he’s not aware of it. <br /><br />Though Little Dorrit is Duty personified—never drudging Duty, always loving Duty—she’s not submissive; and it’s rather amazing that Dickens does get this across, even to modern-day sensibilities. She’s even surprisingly forward at one juncture.<br /><br />Because I am not of the 19th century, when I read this in the 20th century, I loved the character of the bitter Miss Wade. Her articulation of her anger at her lot in life drew me in. Now, in the 21st century, reading Miss Wade’s letter, I didn’t feel the same frisson; but she’s still my favorite of this bunch: a powerful psychological portrait by Dickens. Miss Wade is clearly not Dickens’ favorite, yet he doesn’t punish her as much as the time he was writing in might’ve expected. <br /><br />The last page of the novel is exquisite, a perfect rendering of an oasis of peace amidst a world that will never shut up.<br /><br />(A reread with the Dickens Fellowship of New Orleans)</span>
  <a data-text-id="18043848403731105467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_549960861" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/549960861" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10657252-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341319675p2/10657252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/549960861?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/10657252-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer549960861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2062981103175372865">Good god, was this a snoozer. I love Charles Dickens like nobody's business, but this book was about 600 pages longer than it needed to be. If he was getting paid by the page, I'm not hatin', but it seemed to drag on and on and on without really going anywhere. <br/><br/>Little Dorrit herself is a really boring character because she is a meek little Mary Sue whose entire personality consists of being weak, submissive, and a pushover to everybody else. <br/><br/>The plot is kind of vague and poorly defined and goes </span>
  <span id="freeText2062981103175372865" style="display:none">Good god, was this a snoozer. I love Charles Dickens like nobody's business, but this book was about 600 pages longer than it needed to be. If he was getting paid by the page, I'm not hatin', but it seemed to drag on and on and on without really going anywhere. <br /><br />Little Dorrit herself is a really boring character because she is a meek little Mary Sue whose entire personality consists of being weak, submissive, and a pushover to everybody else. <br /><br />The plot is kind of vague and poorly defined and goes off into weird tangents at times. I finished the book with a few things left unanswered, and the ending felt kind of anticlimactic and rushed (sort of ironic given how the pace dragged the rest of the way).<br /><br />Where this book shines is in Dickens' wonderfully written secondary characters and his brilliant descriptions of an intentionally inept and horribly ineffective bureaucracy. His writing is witty and engaging, and he's quite good at writing memorable characters. <br /><br />I just feel like that wasn't enough to make this book memorable as a whole, though, especially when Dickens has so many fantastic novels. I'd recommend this book to fans of Dickens, but for everybody else, pass on this one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2062981103175372865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Dickens take on the tight rope the masses tried to circumvent to survive, with the dark cloud of possible and quick imprisonment hanging over them all.   <span id="freeText11095709404987469596" style="display:none">My favourite Dickens of them all, and this was just the first time I'd read this! Dickens take on the tight rope the masses tried to circumvent to survive, with the dark cloud of possible and quick imprisonment hanging over them all. A grand tale of fortunes won and lost, that reveals so much about Victorian society.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583312269i/29054348.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Back from overseas, Arthur Clennan takes a kindly interest in his family's seamstress Amy Dorrit, and her father who's in a debtor's prison. Through these relationships Clennan and the reader get to see how the system doesn't work and the significant impact debtors' imprisonment has not only on the families involved, but on society as a whole.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583312269i/29054349._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This is part of a journey I am undertaking to read most of Dickens work, but I was not prepared to find such a gem as this. A wonderful reflection of Dickens in his writing maturity, and hopefully a story for the ages. Splendid! 9 out of 12.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331124599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11329933174845822441">Reading Little Dorrit is like having your own portable fireplace to cozy up to. It’s also huge, like a log or a brick. At 1,000 pages, if you set it on fire, it would burn for a long time. But I don’t mean it that way. I mean reading Little Dorrit makes you want to take off your shoes, don your housecoat and lean way the hell over the open pages, soaking up all that homey tenderness.<br/><br/>Reading Little Dorrit is like suffering the ritual of birthday cake. It’s also enormous like cake is enormous, hea</span>
  <span id="freeText11329933174845822441" style="display:none">Reading Little Dorrit is like having your own portable fireplace to cozy up to. It’s also huge, like a log or a brick. At 1,000 pages, if you set it on fire, it would burn for a long time. But I don’t mean it that way. I mean reading Little Dorrit makes you want to take off your shoes, don your housecoat and lean way the hell over the open pages, soaking up all that homey tenderness.<br /><br />Reading Little Dorrit is like suffering the ritual of birthday cake. It’s also enormous like cake is enormous, heavy and sticky like children’s fingers. But with the ritual I mean watching the cake float towards you in the dark, luminous with spindly candles. You want to lean way the hell over it and, soaking up the glow, make your best wish, blow, and bellyflop into all that icing. <br /><br />Reading Little Dorrit is like being dragged by your parents to a revival festival teeming with tents and strange people. By dragged I mean you used to like going but now think you’re too old for it. You wander around -it’s on the edge of a forest- and you like the smell of the pines and campfires but you stick to the parking lot where some other characters share their six-packs, and there’s a puddle of rain and spew and you lean way the hell over it and see your own reflection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11329933174845822441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it

<div id="review_3565232958" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3565232958" />
    <a title="Brian " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30718608-brian"><img alt="Brian " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567218975p2/30718608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3565232958?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian " href="/user/show/30718608-brian">Brian </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=dickens">dickens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3565232958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3345727208154721082">"No, John, I cannot have you, and I cannot have any husband, it is not my intentions ever to become a wife, it is my intentions always to become a sacrifice, farewell, find another worthy of you, and forget me!"<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1602002034i/30201123._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I don't like to do extensive research before I read books, especially classics. I don't like to start with preconceptions. However, on the back of this copy I have of <i>Little Dorrit</i>, I read something of Dickens that made an impact on the novel. It reads: "Stephen Wall's introduction examin</span>
  <span id="freeText3345727208154721082" style="display:none">"No, John, I cannot have you, and I cannot have any husband, it is not my intentions ever to become a wife, it is my intentions always to become a sacrifice, farewell, find another worthy of you, and forget me!"<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1602002034i/30201123._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I don't like to do extensive research before I read books, especially classics. I don't like to start with preconceptions. However, on the back of this copy I have of <i>Little Dorrit</i>, I read something of Dickens that made an impact on the novel. It reads: "Stephen Wall's introduction examines Dickens's transformation of childhood memories of his father's incarceration in the Marshalsea." I only read that line, not the intro. <br><br>Little Dorrit stays with her father, William Dorrit, who has been incarcerated for debt. Little Dorrit grows up in the prison, and from the environment, becomes a humble girl with a servant's mindset. She lives for the needs of other people. One example: a woman named Maggy, who has had severe brain damage since childhood. Although Little Dorrit has no mother, and has a genuine need, she becomes this woman's mother, takes care of her like a child. Maggy calls Little Dorrit "mother." <br><br>William Dorrit comes upon a financial breakthrough. He gets rich, and gets out of the Marshalsea, goes on into society to live an upper class life. Little Dorrit can't get used to it. She remains the humble girl she had always been in the prison. William tries to fit in, but can't avoid the awkwardness of his years in the prison. <br><br>One day, tragedy comes. Dickens makes the plot twist sudden, and it moved my heart to deep grief. I'm about to spoil it, so if you want to move on or read beyond the next paragraph, you're welcome to do that.<br><br>"And from that hour his poor maimed spirit, only remembering the place where it had broken its wings, cancelled the dream through which it had since groped, and knew of nothing beyond the Marshalsea."<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1460412069i/18737506._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As I read the book, a creative thought came to my mind. I personified grief, and grew angry at the villain. Grief. The anger grew until it overwhelmed me and I saw more than just a creative image. As I followed the anger, I felt deep pain. Yesterday my aunt spoke with me about my father, mother and grandfather passing away and how she missed them. Within seconds I took that same journey Dickens had walked me through: anger, then pain. I only want to point out the literary power of Dickens. A writer who can bring you out of grief-denial has something special.<br><br>I want to mention one more thing about characterization. Dickens can teach how to make characters real. One aspect of his technique I noticed: he gives his characters small quarks that, if you follow, show you something deep about them. For example, the villain Rigaud, throughout the story emerges with his face contorting so that his nose goes over his mustache and his mustache moves up. We see this several times throughout the novel. The genius of this can be experienced. Do it now. Bring your nose over your mouth and move the space above your lips up, and pretend you have a mustache. How do you feel making that face? Now you understand the villain. How brilliant of Dickens!<br><br>Dickens makes my life better. He has a power that strengthens the mind and spirit. If you want to try him, it may take work, but if you can manage to get to a place to understand and enjoy it, you will see the investment worth your time and effort. <br><br>"Be guided only by the healer of the sick, the raiser of the dead, the friend of all who were afflicted and forlorn, the patient Master who shed tears of compassion for our infirmities."<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1602004132i/30201224._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3345727208154721082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_24344398" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24344398" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040930-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250119763p2/1040930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24344398?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/1040930-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040930-laura?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040930-laura?shelf=english-lit">english-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24344398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9177107489374548313">For years I thought this book was some sort of a universal joke, because at the end of Evelyn Waugh's novel, <i>A Handful of Dust</i>, one of the characters ends up trapped in a jungle by a madman who forces the character to read <i>Little Dorrit</i> aloud — I figured this was clearly meant to be a fate worse than death. Turns out, however, that <i>Little Dorrit</i> was merely an appropriate choice because of its themes of imprisonment, delusion, and reversals of fortune. Ah ha!<br/><br/>Little Dorrit (the character) is the d</span>
  <span id="freeText9177107489374548313" style="display:none">For years I thought this book was some sort of a universal joke, because at the end of Evelyn Waugh's novel, <i>A Handful of Dust</i>, one of the characters ends up trapped in a jungle by a madman who forces the character to read <i>Little Dorrit</i> aloud — I figured this was clearly meant to be a fate worse than death. Turns out, however, that <i>Little Dorrit</i> was merely an appropriate choice because of its themes of imprisonment, delusion, and reversals of fortune. Ah ha!<br /><br />Little Dorrit (the character) is the diminutive, angelic daughter of Mr. Dorrit, the “father of the Marshalsea”, which is the debtor’s prison where he resides in a self-manufactured state of importance. Every day while he holds court with the other debtors, his three children leave the Marshalsea to work — a fact nobody ever mentions in order for Mr. Dorrit to maintain the fiction that the Dorrits are people of quality and leisure; their unfortunate 20 year-long incarceration is because of some nebulous financial mismanagement on someone else’s part. <br /><br />Other families with even greater levels of dysfunction and delusion populate this wonderfully rich novel, making the rather daunting 900 pages zip right along. If you’ve read any Dickens, you know to expect plot twists, reversals, dark secrets and convoluted connections — Little Dorrit does not disappoint. As with several of Dickens’ later works that take on various institutions, this novel skewers the penal system and government bureaucracy (with the wonderfully named “Circumlocution Office”). <i>Little Dorrit</i> doesn’t have nearly the same level of intrigue as <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, but Dickens certainly does know how to turn a phrase, and his funniest characterizations never grow old. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9177107489374548313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3170306339" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3170306339" />
    <a title="Haleigh DeRocher " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4013436-haleigh-derocher"><img alt="Haleigh DeRocher " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587613779p2/4013436.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3170306339?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Haleigh DeRocher " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Haleigh DeRocher " href="/user/show/4013436-haleigh-derocher">Haleigh DeRocher </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3170306339" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15149826012578107517">"One always begins to forgive a place as soon as it's been left behind."<br/><br/>Little Dorrit by Charles Dickens, ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐<br/><br/>Before my review of the novel, I'd like to go into a little history. Charles Dickens' father, John, was always a poor manager of his finances - so poor, in fact, that he landed himself in the debtor's prison Marshalsea (sound familiar??). Charles went to work in a boot-blacking factory to help earn money to get his father out of prison, and lived all alone in a run down apartment - at t</span>
  <span id="freeText15149826012578107517" style="display:none">"One always begins to forgive a place as soon as it's been left behind."<br /><br />Little Dorrit by Charles Dickens, ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐<br /><br />Before my review of the novel, I'd like to go into a little history. Charles Dickens' father, John, was always a poor manager of his finances - so poor, in fact, that he landed himself in the debtor's prison Marshalsea (sound familiar??). Charles went to work in a boot-blacking factory to help earn money to get his father out of prison, and lived all alone in a run down apartment - at the meager age of 12. The rest of his family lived in the prison with his father. When he was a child, he always dreamed of living in Gad's Hill Place, a house he often walked by as he went around Kent. Fast forward 30 years - Charles Dickens started writing Little Dorrit (published in serial form between 1955-1957). He visited Marshalsea for the first time since his father was released (which only happened because he received a surprise inheritance - sound familiar???). He purchased Gad's Hill House, his childhood dream! And he continued writing his famously popular stories. Little Dorrit is so much more interesting to me, knowing this history of Charles Dickens. He took so much inspiration from his own life that you can't help but appreciate the novel.<br /><br />Now onto my review. Little Dorrit, a long, elaborate, and sometimes rambling novel, was Dickens' critique on government bureaucracy and British Society as a whole. The Circumlocution office bits were particularly affecting, as were all of the scenes exploring the prison houses. Themes of physical and pyschological imprisonment permeate the narrative.<br /><br />Little Dorrit, also known as Amy, is one of the sweetest literary heroines I have come across in my reading. She is compassionate, self sacrificing, and lives for those she loves. She was born in the Marshalsea Debtor's prison and knows no other life - until her father is released. This turn in fortunes (Book 2) was so interesting to me. To see how the change in fortunes affected the characters really revealed their true spirit. Arthur Clennam, our main protagonist, is also admirable. I loved his integrity and determination. <br /><br />The secondary characters really made the story though. Mysterious Mrs Clennam and all of her dark secrets. Mr Flintwich and Affery. Flora, Tattycorum, Ms Wade, the Meagles, the Beadles - it's such a colorful array of characters! <br /><br />I really enjoyed the mystery, the characters, the social commentary, the beautiful writing, and the touch of romance in this book. Like Bleak House, I hope to read it again someday - and I feel like I will like it even more the second time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15149826012578107517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_514393329" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/514393329" />
    <a title="Feliks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks"><img alt="Feliks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445015906p2/5085729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/514393329?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Feliks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Feliks" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks">Feliks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199596485" href="/book/show/14828052-little-dorrit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5085729-feliks?shelf=favorites-of-all-time">favorites-of-all-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5085729-feliks?shelf=brit-lit-classic">brit-lit-classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer514393329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2406628810310298673">For a long time I languished in the supreme belief that 'Bleak House' was the highest caliber product of Dickens when it came to his 'really big' works. 'Bleak House' is renowned in English literary criticism as--gasp-the #1 novel of the English language. And I too, thought so.<br/><br/>But the difference which makes 'Dorrit' better are these: (1) humor. The book is riotously funny. (2) Better females. The women in 'Bleak House' are melodramatic, traumatic, and oh-so-serious. None of them are really lovab</span>
  <span id="freeText2406628810310298673" style="display:none">For a long time I languished in the supreme belief that 'Bleak House' was the highest caliber product of Dickens when it came to his 'really big' works. 'Bleak House' is renowned in English literary criticism as--gasp-the #1 novel of the English language. And I too, thought so.<br /><br />But the difference which makes 'Dorrit' better are these: (1) humor. The book is riotously funny. (2) Better females. The women in 'Bleak House' are melodramatic, traumatic, and oh-so-serious. None of them are really lovable. But Amy Dorrit is adorable! (3) Arthur Clenman. For once, Dickens gives us a fully-fledged, sensitive male character to whom any regular guy can identify with. He is not lurid, overly-virtuous, overly-heroic, or exaggerated. <br /><br />Anyway. This novel is one of Dickens' career best. It displays the caliber of writing you can always see from Dickens when he's really 'in-the-zone'. <br /><br />I would match it against any of his other works except 'A Tale of Two Cities' which is in a class of its own. But 'Dorrit' vs 'Copperfield', or 'Expectations', or 'Nickelby', or 'Mutual Friend' or 'Pickwick'...or even 'Bleak'(!) certainly 'Dorrit' blows them away. Its astounding but so. This is one case where you can't listen to the critics: no matter how much one respects 'Bleak'; 'Dorrit' is a narrative which will bring exuberation and good cheer to your life. <br /><br />Every Dickens novel has fun villains to despise--this one has them too-- but for once here is a novel with humor and sweetness, and characters you can really take to your heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2406628810310298673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

N now he think he hot shit jus cus all da prisoners look up 2 him. n he always thinks his kids don work (but dey do). he is off his wacker n shiyt, nom sayin? so dis guy arthur think he owes dees dorrit peeps bc his pops was into sum shady shyt or wh</span>
  <span id="freeText95463746216147035" style="display:none">from da scorchin sun a marsellies 2 da dark cold cellof a debtors prison, lill dorrit b 1 of dickens 4gotten masta pieces.<br /><br />dey be lockin boyz up 4 sum wack shit back in da day. ma man dorrit wuz in jail 4 debt 4 so long he had 3 dam kids up in there. N now he think he hot shit jus cus all da prisoners look up 2 him. n he always thinks his kids don work (but dey do). he is off his wacker n shiyt, nom sayin? so dis guy arthur think he owes dees dorrit peeps bc his pops was into sum shady shyt or whateva. N lil dorrit crushin on arthur hard, but arthur aint into her like dat. she seem pretty plain, but sum dig her so maybe she fine. her sista seem fine doe. maybe lil got a tite body but aint much to look at. or maybe da other fellas chechin out her sis. dont really noe.<br /><br />anyway, book was pretty legit, doe i wish lil d wuz finer. she probly alright doe. deres also dis thing called da circumlocution office. mad funni satire bro!!!!!! 1 dey be sayin gov cant do shit. i bet sum obama h8ers wrote dem chapters. sic dis book is old.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8QD5CzJnZzmgnVpOQsavpccneRZzy6JH0OyjNQm2VNv82jAQdAm7C5gzay//7hL4wdT0R7kjS5RQ1gRdzP57/Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('m+jjfrI9HBWHmzs2Kd3bDwQCoiWFq3tm447pIxC4wt+WMipl9FPAJ781CleU9WZSAvEvdE9DkrVjtE5L1fDt+Q==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

One is the shy Caroline Helstone, who is trapped in the oppressive atmosphere of a Yorkshire rectory and whose bare life symbolizes the plight of single women in the nineteenth century. The other is the vivacious Shirley Keeldar, who inherits a local estate and whose wealth liberates her from convention.A work that combines social commentary with the more private preoccupations of Jane Eyre, Shirley demonstrates the full range of Brontë\'s literary talent. &quot;Shirley is a revolutionary novel,&quot; wrote Brontë biographer Lyndall Gordon. &quot;Shirley follows Jane Eyre as a new exemplar but so much a forerunner of the feminist of the later twentieth century that it is hard to believe in her actual existence in 1811-12. She is a theoretic possibility: what a woman might be if she combined independence and means of her own with intellect. Charlotte Brontë imagined a new form of power, equal to that of men, in a confident young woman [whose] extraordinary freedom has accustomed her to think for herself....Shirley [is] Brontë\'s most Soon after Sir Thomas absents himself on estate business in Antigua (the family\'s investment in slavery and sugar is considered in the Introduction in a new, post-colonial light), Mary Crawford and her brother Henry arrive at Mansfield, bringing with them London glamour, and the seductive taste for flirtation and theatre that precipitates a crisis.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">About Charles Dickens</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1387078070p3/239579.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">Charles Dickens
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
23,206
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2493348614266398093"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br/><br/>Dicke</span>
  <span id="freeText2493348614266398093" style="display:none"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br /><br />Dickens left school to work in a factory when his father was incarcerated in a debtors' prison. Despite his lack of formal education, he edited a weekly journal for 20 years, wrote 15 novels, five novellas, hundreds of short stories and non-fiction articles, lectured and performed extensively, was an indefatigable letter writer, and campaigned vigorously for children's rights, education, and other social reforms.<br /><br />Dickens was regarded as the literary colossus of his age. His 1843 novella, <i>A Christmas Carol</i>, remains popular and continues to inspire adaptations in every artistic genre. <i>Oliver Twist</i> and <i>Great Expectations</i> are also frequently adapted, and, like many of his novels, evoke images of early Victorian London. His 1859 novel, <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, set in London and Paris, is his best-known work of historical fiction. Dickens's creative genius has been praised by fellow writers—from Leo Tolstoy to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> and G. K. Chesterton—for its realism, comedy, prose style, unique characterisations, and social criticism. On the other hand, Oscar Wilde, Henry James, and Virginia Woolf complained of a lack of psychological depth, loose writing, and a vein of saccharine sentimentalism. The term Dickensian is used to describe something that is reminiscent of Dickens and his writings, such as poor social conditions or comically repulsive characters.<br /><br />On 8 June 1870, Dickens suffered another stroke at his home after a full day's work on <i>Edwin Drood.</i> He never regained consciousness, and the next day he died at Gad's Hill Place. Contrary to his wish to be buried at Rochester Cathedral "in an inexpensive, unostentatious, and strictly private manner," he was laid to rest in the Poets' Corner of Westminster Abbey. A printed epitaph circulated at the time of the funeral reads: "To the Memory of Charles Dickens (England's most popular author) who died at his residence, Higham, near Rochester, Kent, 9 June 1870, aged 58 years. He was a sympathiser with the poor, the suffering, and the oppressed; and by his death, one of England's greatest writers is lost to the world." His last words were: "On the ground", in response to his sister-in-law Georgina's request that he lie down.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2493348614266398093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
